<li><a href="/wiki/Health_technology" title="Health technology">Health technology</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pharming_(genetics)" title="Pharming (genetics)"> Pharming</a></li></ul></div></div></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-below hlist">
<ul><li><span class="nowrap"><span class="nowrap"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Issoria_lathonia.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="icon" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Issoria_lathonia.jpg/20px-Issoria_lathonia.jpg" decoding="async" width="17" height="12" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Issoria_lathonia.jpg/40px-Issoria_lathonia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="629" data-file-height="445" /></a></span> </span><a href="/wiki/Portal:Biology" title="Portal:Biology">Biology&#32;portal</a></span></li>
<li><span class="nowrap"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><span title="Category"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/20px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/96/Symbol_category_class.svg/40px-Symbol_category_class.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="185" /></span></span>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Category:Biology" title="Category:Biology">Category</a></span></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1333133064" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:TopicTOC-Biology" title="Template:TopicTOC-Biology"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:TopicTOC-Biology" title="Template talk:TopicTOC-Biology"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:TopicTOC-Biology" title="Special:EditPage/Template:TopicTOC-Biology"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Biotechnology encompasses a wide range of procedures for <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">modifying</a> living <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> for human purposes, going back to <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domestication</a> of animals, the cultivation of plants, and "improvements" to these through breeding programs that employ artificial selection and <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridization</a>. Modern usage also includes genetic engineering, as well as <a href="/wiki/Cell_culture" title="Cell culture">cell</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tissue_culture" title="Tissue culture">tissue culture</a> technologies. The <a href="/wiki/American_Chemical_Society" title="American Chemical Society">American Chemical Society</a> defines <i>biotechnology</i> as the application of biological organisms, systems, or processes by various industries to learning about the <a href="/wiki/List_of_life_sciences" title="List of life sciences">science of life</a> and the improvement of the value of materials and organisms, such as pharmaceuticals, crops, and <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As per the <a href="/wiki/European_Federation_of_Biotechnology" title="European Federation of Biotechnology">European Federation of Biotechnology</a>, biotechnology is the integration of natural science and organisms, cells, parts thereof, and molecular analogues for products and services.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Biotechnology is based on the <a href="/wiki/Basic_research" title="Basic research">basic</a> <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biological sciences</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Molecular_biology" title="Molecular biology">molecular biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biochemistry" title="Biochemistry">biochemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cell_biology" title="Cell biology">cell biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Embryology" title="Embryology">embryology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a>) and conversely provides methods to support and perform basic research in biology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Tissue_engineering_english.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="A visual representation of tissue engineering principles, demonstrating the creation of functional tissues using a combination of engineering and biological concepts" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Tissue_engineering_english.jpg/250px-Tissue_engineering_english.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Tissue_engineering_english.jpg/500px-Tissue_engineering_english.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a><figcaption>Principles of Tissue Engineering</figcaption></figure>
<p>Biotechnology is the <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">research and development</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Laboratory" title="Laboratory">laboratory</a> using <a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">bioinformatics</a> for exploration, extraction, exploitation, and production from any <a href="/wiki/Living_organisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Living organisms">living organisms</a> and any source of <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a> by means of <a href="/wiki/Biochemical_engineering" title="Biochemical engineering">biochemical engineering</a> where high value-added products could be planned (reproduced by <a href="/wiki/Biosynthesis" title="Biosynthesis">biosynthesis</a>, for example), forecasted, formulated, developed, manufactured, and marketed for the purpose of sustainable operations (for the return from bottomless initial investment on R &amp; D) and gaining durable patents rights (for exclusives rights for sales, and prior to this to receive national and international approval from the results on animal experiment and human experiment, especially on the <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceutical</a> branch of biotechnology to prevent any undetected side-effects or safety concerns by using the products).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The utilization of biological processes, <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> or systems to produce products that are anticipated to improve human lives is termed biotechnology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>By contrast, <a href="/wiki/Bioengineering" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioengineering">bioengineering</a> is generally thought of as a related field that more heavily emphasizes higher systems approaches (not necessarily the altering or using of biological materials <i>directly</i>) for interfacing with and utilizing living things. Bioengineering is the application of the principles of <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">engineering</a> and natural sciences to tissues, cells, and molecules. This can be considered as the use of knowledge from working with and manipulating biology to achieve a result that can improve functions in plants and animals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Relatedly, <a href="/wiki/Biomedical_engineering" title="Biomedical engineering">biomedical engineering</a> is an overlapping field that often draws upon and applies <i>biotechnology</i> (by various definitions), especially in certain sub-fields of biomedical or <a href="/wiki/Chemical_engineering" title="Chemical engineering">chemical engineering</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Tissue_engineering" title="Tissue engineering">tissue engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_engineering" title="Pharmaceutical engineering">biopharmaceutical engineering</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biotechnology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_biotechnology" title="History of biotechnology">History of biotechnology</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Brewer_designed_and_engraved_in_the_Sixteenth._Century_by_J_Amman.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/The_Brewer_designed_and_engraved_in_the_Sixteenth._Century_by_J_Amman.png/250px-The_Brewer_designed_and_engraved_in_the_Sixteenth._Century_by_J_Amman.png" decoding="async" width="180" height="229" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/The_Brewer_designed_and_engraved_in_the_Sixteenth._Century_by_J_Amman.png/500px-The_Brewer_designed_and_engraved_in_the_Sixteenth._Century_by_J_Amman.png 2x" data-file-width="723" data-file-height="921" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Brewing" title="Brewing">Brewing</a> was an early application of biotechnology.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Many forms of human-derived <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> fit the broad definition of "utilizing a biotechnological system to make products". The cultivation of plants may be viewed as the earliest biotechnological enterprise.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Agriculture has been theorized to have become the dominant way of producing food since the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a>. Through early biotechnology, the earliest farmers selected and bred the best-suited crops (e.g., those with the highest yields) to produce enough food to support a growing population. As crops and fields became increasingly large and difficult to maintain, it was discovered that specific organisms and their by-products could effectively <a href="/wiki/Fertilize" class="mw-redirect" title="Fertilize">fertilize</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_fixation" title="Nitrogen fixation">restore nitrogen</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">control pests</a>. Throughout the history of agriculture, farmers have inadvertently altered the genetics of their crops through introducing them to new environments and <a href="/wiki/Plant_breeding" title="Plant breeding">breeding</a> them with other plants — one of the first forms of biotechnology.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (February 2022)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>These processes were also included in the early fermentation of <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These processes were introduced in early <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a> and <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, and still use the same basic biological methods. In <a href="/wiki/Brewing" title="Brewing">brewing</a>, malted grains (containing <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>) convert starch from grains into sugar and then adding specific <a href="/wiki/Yeast" title="Yeast">yeasts</a> to produce beer. In this process, <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> in the grains broke down into alcohols, such as ethanol. Later, other cultures developed the process of <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid_fermentation" title="Lactic acid fermentation">lactic acid fermentation</a>, which produced other preserved foods, such as <a href="/wiki/Soy_sauce" title="Soy sauce">soy sauce</a>. Fermentation was also used in this time period to produce <a href="/wiki/Leavened_bread" class="mw-redirect" title="Leavened bread">leavened bread</a>. Although the process of fermentation was not fully understood until <a href="/wiki/Louis_Pasteur" title="Louis Pasteur">Louis Pasteur</a>'s work in 1857, it is still the first use of biotechnology to convert a food source into another form.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Before the time of <a href="/wiki/Charles_Darwin" title="Charles Darwin">Charles Darwin</a>'s work and life, animal and plant scientists had already used selective breeding. Darwin added to that body of work with his scientific observations about the ability of science to change species. These accounts contributed to Darwin's theory of natural selection.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>For thousands of years, humans have used selective breeding to improve the production of crops and livestock to use them for food. In selective breeding, organisms with desirable characteristics are mated to produce offspring with the same characteristics. For example, this technique was used with corn to produce the largest and sweetest crops.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Thieman&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thieman-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In the early twentieth century scientists gained a greater understanding of <a href="/wiki/Microbiology" title="Microbiology">microbiology</a> and explored ways of manufacturing specific products. In 1917, <a href="/wiki/Chaim_Weizmann" title="Chaim Weizmann">Chaim Weizmann</a> first used a pure microbiological culture in an industrial process, that of manufacturing <a href="/wiki/Corn_starch" title="Corn starch">corn starch</a> using <i><a href="/wiki/Clostridium_acetobutylicum" title="Clostridium acetobutylicum">Clostridium acetobutylicum</a>,</i> to produce <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a>, which the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> desperately needed to manufacture <a href="/wiki/Explosive" title="Explosive">explosives</a> during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Springham&#95;biotechnology&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Springham_biotechnology-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Biotechnology has also led to the development of antibiotics. In 1928, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Fleming" title="Alexander Fleming">Alexander Fleming</a> discovered the mold <i><a href="/wiki/Penicillium" title="Penicillium">Penicillium</a></i>. His work led to the purification of the antibiotic formed by the mold by <a href="/wiki/Howard_Florey" title="Howard Florey">Howard Florey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Boris_Chain" class="mw-redirect" title="Ernst Boris Chain">Ernst Boris Chain</a> and <a href="/wiki/Norman_Heatley" title="Norman Heatley">Norman Heatley</a> – to form what we today know as <a href="/wiki/Penicillin" title="Penicillin">penicillin</a>. In 1940, penicillin became available for medicinal use to treat bacterial infections in humans.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Thieman&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thieman-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The field of modern biotechnology is generally thought of as having been born in 1971 when Paul Berg's (Stanford) experiments in gene splicing had early success. <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Boyer" title="Herbert Boyer">Herbert W. Boyer</a> (Univ. Calif. at San Francisco) and <a href="/wiki/Stanley_Norman_Cohen" title="Stanley Norman Cohen">Stanley N. Cohen</a> (Stanford) significantly advanced the new technology in 1972 by transferring genetic material into a bacterium, such that the imported material would be reproduced. The commercial viability of a biotechnology industry was significantly expanded on June 16, 1980, when the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Supreme_Court" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Supreme Court">United States Supreme Court</a> ruled that a <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetically modified</a> <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganism</a> could be <a href="/wiki/Patent" title="Patent">patented</a> in the case of <i><a href="/wiki/Diamond_v._Chakrabarty" title="Diamond v. Chakrabarty">Diamond v. Chakrabarty</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DiamondvChakrabarty&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DiamondvChakrabarty-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Indian-born <a href="/wiki/Ananda_Mohan_Chakrabarty" title="Ananda Mohan Chakrabarty">Ananda Chakrabarty</a>, working for <a href="/wiki/General_Electric" title="General Electric">General Electric</a>, had modified a bacterium (of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Pseudomonas" title="Pseudomonas">Pseudomonas</a></i>) capable of breaking down crude oil, which he proposed to use in treating oil spills. (Chakrabarty's work did not involve gene manipulation but rather the transfer of entire organelles between strains of the <i>Pseudomonas</i> bacterium).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/MOSFET" title="MOSFET">MOSFET</a> was invented at Bell Labs between 1955 and 1960,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:02&#95;20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:02-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lojek1202&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lojek1202-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Two years later, <a href="/wiki/Leland_C._Clark" class="mw-redirect" title="Leland C. Clark">Leland C. Clark</a> and Champ Lyons invented the first <a href="/wiki/Biosensor" title="Biosensor">biosensor</a> in 1962.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Park&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Park-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bio-FET" title="Bio-FET">Biosensor MOSFETs</a> were later developed, and they have since been widely used to measure <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">chemical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biological" class="mw-redirect" title="Biological">biological</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">environmental</a> parameters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bergveld&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergveld-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The first BioFET was the <a href="/wiki/Ion-sensitive_field-effect_transistor" class="mw-redirect" title="Ion-sensitive field-effect transistor">ion-sensitive field-effect transistor</a> (ISFET), invented by <a href="/wiki/Piet_Bergveld" title="Piet Bergveld">Piet Bergveld</a> in 1970.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bergveld1970&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergveld1970-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is a special type of MOSFET,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bergveld&#95;27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergveld-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> where the <a href="/wiki/Metal_gate" title="Metal gate">metal gate</a> is replaced by an <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ion</a>-sensitive <a href="/wiki/Membrane" title="Membrane">membrane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrolyte" title="Electrolyte">electrolyte</a> solution and <a href="/wiki/Reference_electrode" title="Reference electrode">reference electrode</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schoning&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schoning-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The ISFET is widely used in <a href="/wiki/Biomedical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biomedical">biomedical</a> applications, such as the detection of <a href="/wiki/DNA_hybridization" class="mw-redirect" title="DNA hybridization">DNA hybridization</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biomarker" title="Biomarker">biomarker</a> detection from <a href="/wiki/Blood" title="Blood">blood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antibody" title="Antibody">antibody</a> detection, <a href="/wiki/Glucose" title="Glucose">glucose</a> measurement, <a href="/wiki/PH" title="PH">pH</a> sensing, and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_technology" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetic technology">genetic technology</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schoning&#95;30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schoning-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>By the mid-1980s, other BioFETs had been developed, including the <a href="/wiki/Gas_sensor" class="mw-redirect" title="Gas sensor">gas sensor</a> FET (GASFET), <a href="/wiki/Pressure_sensor" class="mw-redirect" title="Pressure sensor">pressure sensor</a> FET (PRESSFET), <a href="/wiki/Chemical_field-effect_transistor" title="Chemical field-effect transistor">chemical field-effect transistor</a> (ChemFET), <a href="/wiki/ISFET" title="ISFET">reference ISFET</a> (REFET), enzyme-modified FET (ENFET) and immunologically modified FET (IMFET).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bergveld&#95;27-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bergveld-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By the early 2000s, BioFETs such as the <a href="/wiki/DNA_field-effect_transistor" title="DNA field-effect transistor">DNA field-effect transistor</a> (DNAFET), <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetically modified">gene-modified</a> FET (GenFET) and <a href="/wiki/Membrane_potential" title="Membrane potential">cell-potential</a> BioFET (CPFET) had been developed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Schoning&#95;30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schoning-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A factor influencing the biotechnology sector's success is improved intellectual property rights legislation—and enforcement—worldwide, as well as strengthened demand for medical and pharmaceutical products.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Rising demand for biofuels is expected to be good news for the biotechnology sector, with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Energy" title="United States Department of Energy">Department of Energy</a> estimating <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> usage could reduce U.S. petroleum-derived fuel consumption by up to 30% by 2030. The biotechnology sector has allowed the U.S. farming industry to rapidly increase its supply of corn and soybeans—the main inputs into biofuels—by developing genetically modified seeds that resist pests and drought. By increasing farm productivity, biotechnology boosts biofuel production.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Examples">Examples</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biotechnology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Examples"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_biotechnology" title="Outline of biotechnology">Outline of biotechnology</a></div>
<p>Biotechnology has applications in four major industrial areas, including health care (medical), crop production and agriculture, non-food (industrial) uses of crops and other products (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Biodegradable_plastic" class="mw-redirect" title="Biodegradable plastic">biodegradable plastics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">vegetable oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>), and <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environmental</a> uses.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>For example, one application of biotechnology is the directed use of <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> for the manufacture of organic products (examples include <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Milk" title="Milk">milk</a> products). Another example is using naturally present <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacteria</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Mining_industry" class="mw-redirect" title="Mining industry">mining industry</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bioleaching" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioleaching">bioleaching</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Biotechnology is also used to recycle, treat waste, clean up sites contaminated by industrial activities (<a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a>), and also to produce <a href="/wiki/Biological_warfare" title="Biological warfare">biological weapons</a>.
</p><p>A series of derived terms have been coined to identify several branches of biotechnology, for example:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bioinformatics" title="Bioinformatics">Bioinformatics</a> (or "gold biotechnology") is an interdisciplinary field that addresses biological problems using computational techniques, and makes the rapid organization as well as analysis of biological data possible. The field may also be referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Computational_biology" title="Computational biology">computational biology</a></i>, and can be defined as, "conceptualizing biology in terms of molecules and then applying informatics techniques to understand and organize the information associated with these molecules, on a large scale".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-gerstein&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gerstein-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bioinformatics plays a key role in various areas, such as <a href="/wiki/Functional_genomics" title="Functional genomics">functional genomics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structural_genomics" title="Structural genomics">structural genomics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Proteomics" title="Proteomics">proteomics</a>, and forms a key component in the biotechnology and pharmaceutical sector.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:2&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Blue biotechnology is based on the exploitation of sea resources to create products and industrial applications.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This branch of biotechnology is the most used for the industries of refining and combustion principally on the production of <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">bio-oils</a> with photosynthetic micro-algae.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:1&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Green biotechnology is biotechnology applied to agricultural processes. An example would be the selection and domestication of plants via <a href="/wiki/Micropropagation" title="Micropropagation">micropropagation</a>. Another example is the designing of <a href="/wiki/Transgenic_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Transgenic plant">transgenic plants</a> to grow under specific environments in the presence (or absence) of chemicals. One hope is that green biotechnology might produce more environmentally friendly solutions than traditional <a href="/wiki/Industrial_agriculture" title="Industrial agriculture">industrial agriculture</a>. An example of this is the engineering of a plant to express a <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a>, thereby ending the need of external application of pesticides. An example of this would be <a href="/wiki/Transgenic_maize" class="mw-redirect" title="Transgenic maize">Bt corn</a>. Whether or not green biotechnology products such as this are ultimately more environmentally friendly is a topic of considerable debate.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is commonly considered as the next phase of green revolution, which can be seen as a platform to eradicate world hunger by using technologies which enable the production of more fertile and resistant, towards <a href="/wiki/Biotic_stress" title="Biotic stress">biotic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Abiotic_stress" title="Abiotic stress">abiotic stress</a>, plants and ensures application of environmentally friendly fertilizers and the use of biopesticides, it is mainly focused on the development of agriculture.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> On the other hand, some of the uses of green biotechnology involve <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a> to clean and reduce waste.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Red biotechnology is the use of biotechnology in the medical and <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical">pharmaceutical</a> industries, and health preservation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This branch involves the production of <a href="/wiki/Vaccine" title="Vaccine">vaccines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotics</a>, regenerative therapies, creation of artificial organs and new diagnostics of diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As well as the development of <a href="/wiki/Hormones" class="mw-redirect" title="Hormones">hormones</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stem_cells" class="mw-redirect" title="Stem cells">stem cells</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antibodies" class="mw-redirect" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a>, siRNA and <a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_tests" class="mw-redirect" title="Diagnostic tests">diagnostic tests</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>White biotechnology, also known as industrial biotechnology, is biotechnology applied to <a href="/wiki/Manufacturing" title="Manufacturing">industrial</a> processes. An example is the designing of an organism to produce a useful chemical. Another example is the using of <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a> as industrial <a href="/wiki/Catalyst" class="mw-redirect" title="Catalyst">catalysts</a> to either produce valuable chemicals or destroy hazardous/polluting chemicals. White biotechnology tends to consume less in resources than traditional processes used to produce industrial goods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:4&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Yellow biotechnology refers to the use of biotechnology in food production (<a href="/wiki/Food_industry" title="Food industry">food industry</a>), for example in making wine (<a href="/wiki/Winemaking" title="Winemaking">winemaking</a>), cheese (<a href="/wiki/Cheesemaking" title="Cheesemaking">cheesemaking</a>), and beer (<a href="/wiki/Brewing" title="Brewing">brewing</a>) by <a href="/wiki/Fermentation" title="Fermentation">fermentation</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has also been used to refer to biotechnology applied to insects. This includes biotechnology-based approaches for the control of harmful insects, the characterisation and utilisation of active ingredients or genes of insects for research, or application in agriculture and medicine and various other approaches.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:6&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Gray biotechnology is dedicated to environmental applications, and focused on the maintenance of <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> and the remotion of pollutants.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Brown biotechnology is related to the management of arid lands and <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">deserts</a>. One application is the creation of enhanced seeds that resist extreme <a href="/wiki/Desert_climate" title="Desert climate">environmental conditions</a> of arid regions, which is related to the innovation, creation of agriculture techniques and management of resources.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Violet biotechnology is related to law, ethical and philosophical issues around biotechnology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Microbial biotechnology has been proposed for the rapidly emerging area of biotechnology applications in space and microgravity (space bioeconomy)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-space&#95;43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-space-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li>
<li>Dark biotechnology is the color associated with <a href="/wiki/Bioterrorism" title="Bioterrorism">bioterrorism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Biological_agent" title="Biological agent">biological weapons</a> and biowarfare which uses microorganisms, and toxins to cause diseases and death in humans, livestock and crops.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-:0&#95;37-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Medicine">Medicine</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biotechnology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medicine"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In medicine, modern biotechnology has many applications in areas such as <a href="/wiki/Pharmaceutical_drug" class="mw-redirect" title="Pharmaceutical drug">pharmaceutical drug</a> discoveries and production, <a href="/wiki/Pharmacogenomics" title="Pharmacogenomics">pharmacogenomics</a>, and genetic testing (or <a href="/wiki/Genetic_testing" title="Genetic testing">genetic screening</a>). In 2021, nearly 40% of the total company value of pharmaceutical biotech companies worldwide were active in <a href="/wiki/Oncology" title="Oncology">Oncology</a> with <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">Neurology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rare_Disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Rare Disease">Rare Diseases</a> being the other two big applications.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Microarray2.gif" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Microarray2.gif/250px-Microarray2.gif" decoding="async" width="250" height="152" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Microarray2.gif/500px-Microarray2.gif 2x" data-file-width="835" data-file-height="507" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/DNA_microarray" title="DNA microarray">DNA microarray</a> chip – some can do as many as a million blood tests at once. </figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pharmacogenomics" title="Pharmacogenomics">Pharmacogenomics</a> (a combination of <a href="/wiki/Pharmacology" title="Pharmacology">pharmacology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genomics" title="Genomics">genomics</a>) is the technology that analyses how genetic makeup affects an individual's response to drugs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Researchers in the field investigate the influence of <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> variation on drug responses in patients by correlating <a href="/wiki/Gene_expression" title="Gene expression">gene expression</a> or <a href="/wiki/Single-nucleotide_polymorphism" title="Single-nucleotide polymorphism">single-nucleotide polymorphisms</a> with a drug's <a href="/wiki/Efficacy" title="Efficacy">efficacy</a> or <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid20836007&#95;47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20836007-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The purpose of pharmacogenomics is to develop rational means to optimize drug therapy, with respect to the patients' <a href="/wiki/Genotype" title="Genotype">genotype</a>, to ensure maximum efficacy with minimal <a href="/wiki/Adverse_effect_(medicine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Adverse effect (medicine)">adverse effects</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid19530963&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19530963-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Such approaches promise the advent of "<a href="/wiki/Personalized_medicine" title="Personalized medicine">personalized medicine</a>"; in which drugs and drug combinations are optimized for each individual's unique genetic makeup.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-pmid20712531&#95;50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pmid20712531-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:InsulinHexamer.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/InsulinHexamer.jpg/250px-InsulinHexamer.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="262" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/InsulinHexamer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="380" data-file-height="398" /></a><figcaption>Computer-generated image of insulin hexamers highlighting the threefold <a href="/wiki/Symmetry" title="Symmetry">symmetry</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Zinc" title="Zinc">zinc</a> ions holding it together, and the <a href="/wiki/Histidine" title="Histidine">histidine</a> residues involved in zinc binding</figcaption></figure>
<p>Biotechnology has contributed to the discovery and manufacturing of traditional <a href="/wiki/Small_molecule" title="Small molecule">small molecule</a> pharmaceutical drugs as well as drugs that are the product of biotechnology – <a href="/wiki/Biopharmaceutics" class="mw-redirect" title="Biopharmaceutics">biopharmaceutics</a>. Modern biotechnology can be used to manufacture existing medicines relatively easily and cheaply. The first genetically engineered products were medicines designed to treat human diseases. To cite one example, in 1978 <a href="/wiki/Genentech" title="Genentech">Genentech</a> developed synthetic humanized <a href="/wiki/Insulin" title="Insulin">insulin</a> by joining its gene with a <a href="/wiki/Plasmid" title="Plasmid">plasmid</a> vector inserted into the bacterium <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i>. Insulin, widely used for the treatment of diabetes, was previously extracted from the pancreas of <a href="/wiki/Abattoir" class="mw-redirect" title="Abattoir">abattoir</a> animals (cattle or pigs). The genetically engineered bacteria are able to produce large quantities of synthetic human insulin at relatively low cost.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-USIS&#95;52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USIS-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Biotechnology has also enabled emerging therapeutics like <a href="/wiki/Gene_therapy" title="Gene therapy">gene therapy</a>. The application of biotechnology to basic science (for example through the <a href="/wiki/Human_Genome_Project" title="Human Genome Project">Human Genome Project</a>) has also dramatically improved our understanding of <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a> and as our scientific knowledge of normal and disease biology has increased, our ability to develop new medicines to treat previously untreatable diseases has increased as well.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-USIS&#95;52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USIS-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genetic_testing" title="Genetic testing">Genetic testing</a> allows the <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetic</a> <a href="/wiki/Medical_diagnosis" title="Medical diagnosis">diagnosis</a> of vulnerabilities to inherited <a href="/wiki/Diseases" class="mw-redirect" title="Diseases">diseases</a>, and can also be used to determine a child's parentage (genetic mother and father) or in general a person's <a href="/wiki/Ancestry" class="mw-redirect" title="Ancestry">ancestry</a>. In addition to studying <a href="/wiki/Chromosomes" class="mw-redirect" title="Chromosomes">chromosomes</a> to the level of individual genes, genetic testing in a broader sense includes <a href="/wiki/Biochemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biochemical">biochemical</a> tests for the possible presence of genetic diseases, or mutant forms of genes associated with increased risk of developing genetic disorders. Genetic testing identifies changes in <a href="/wiki/Chromosomes" class="mw-redirect" title="Chromosomes">chromosomes</a>, genes, or proteins.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Most of the time, testing is used to find changes that are associated with inherited disorders. The results of a genetic test can confirm or rule out a suspected genetic condition or help determine a person's chance of developing or passing on a <a href="/wiki/Genetic_disorder" title="Genetic disorder">genetic disorder</a>. As of 2011 several hundred genetic tests were in use.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since genetic testing may open up ethical or psychological problems, genetic testing is often accompanied by <a href="/wiki/Genetic_counseling" title="Genetic counseling">genetic counseling</a>.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Agriculture">Agriculture</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biotechnology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Agriculture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Agricultural_biotechnology" title="Agricultural biotechnology">Agricultural biotechnology</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">Genetically modified crops</a> ("GM crops", or "biotech crops") are plants used in <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> of which has been modified with <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> techniques. In most cases, the main aim is to introduce a new <a href="/wiki/Trait_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Trait (biology)">trait</a> that does not occur naturally in the species. Biotechnology firms can contribute to future food security by improving the nutrition and viability of <a href="/wiki/Urban_agriculture" title="Urban agriculture">urban agriculture</a>. Furthermore, the protection of intellectual property rights encourages private sector investment in agrobiotechnology.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Examples in food crops include resistance to certain pests,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-news.google.co.uk&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.google.co.uk-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> diseases,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> stressful environmental conditions,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> resistance to chemical treatments (e.g. resistance to a <a href="/wiki/Herbicide" title="Herbicide">herbicide</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), reduction of spoilage,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Haroldsen1&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haroldsen1-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or improving the nutrient profile of the crop.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Examples in non-food crops include production of <a href="/wiki/Plant_manufactured_pharmaceuticals" title="Plant manufactured pharmaceuticals">pharmaceutical agents</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and other industrially useful goods,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> as well as for <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Diaz&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Diaz-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Farmers have widely adopted GM technology. Between 1996 and 2011, the total surface area of land cultivated with GM crops had increased by a factor of 94, from 17,000 to 1,600,000 square kilometers (4,200,000 to 395,400,000 acres).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-James2011&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James2011-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 10% of the world's crop lands were planted with GM crops in 2010.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-James2011&#95;68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James2011-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As of 2011, 11 different transgenic crops were grown commercially on 395&#160;million acres (160&#160;million hectares) in 29 countries such as the US, <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">Argentina</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, Canada, China, Paraguay, Pakistan, South Africa, Uruguay, Bolivia, Australia, Philippines, Myanmar, Burkina Faso, Mexico, and Spain.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-James2011&#95;68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James2011-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_food" title="Genetically modified food">Genetically modified foods</a> are foods produced from <a href="/wiki/Organism" title="Organism">organisms</a> that have had specific changes introduced into their <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> with the methods of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a>. These techniques have allowed for the introduction of new crop traits as well as a far greater control over a food's genetic structure than previously afforded by methods such as <a href="/wiki/Selective_breeding" title="Selective breeding">selective breeding</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mutation_breeding" title="Mutation breeding">mutation breeding</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Commercial sale of genetically modified foods began in 1994, when <a href="/wiki/Calgene" class="mw-redirect" title="Calgene">Calgene</a> first marketed its <a href="/wiki/Flavr_Savr" title="Flavr Savr">Flavr Savr</a> delayed ripening tomato.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-James&#95;1996&#95;70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James_1996-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To date most genetic modification of foods have primarily focused on <a href="/wiki/Cash_crop" title="Cash crop">cash crops</a> in high demand by farmers such as <a href="/wiki/Transgenic_soybean" class="mw-redirect" title="Transgenic soybean">soybean</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transgenic_maize" class="mw-redirect" title="Transgenic maize">corn</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canola" class="mw-redirect" title="Canola">canola</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cotton_seed_oil" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotton seed oil">cotton seed oil</a>. These have been engineered for resistance to pathogens and herbicides and better nutrient profiles. GM livestock have also been experimentally developed; in November 2013 none were available on the market,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but in 2015 the FDA approved the first GM salmon for commercial production and consumption.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There is a <a href="/wiki/Scientific_consensus" title="Scientific consensus">scientific consensus</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nicolia2013&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nicolia2013-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FAO&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAO-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ronald2011&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ronald2011-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Also&#95;76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Also-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> that currently available food derived from GM crops poses no greater risk to human health than conventional food,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-AAAS2012&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AAAS2012-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ECom2010&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ECom2010-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-AMA2001&#95;79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMA2001-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-LoC2015&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LoC2015-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-NAS2016&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NAS2016-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but that each GM food needs to be tested on a case-by-case basis before introduction.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-WHOFAQ&#95;82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHOFAQ-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Haslberger2003&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Haslberger2003-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-BMA2004&#95;84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BMA2004-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nonetheless, members of the public are much less likely than scientists to perceive GM foods as safe.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-PEW2015&#95;85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PEW2015-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Marris2001&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Marris2001-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-PABE&#95;87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PABE-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Scott2016&#95;88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott2016-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The legal and regulatory status of GM foods varies by country, with some nations banning or restricting them, and others permitting them with widely differing degrees of regulation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-loc.gov&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-loc.gov-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bashshur&#95;90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bashshur-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sifferlin&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sifferlin-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Council&#95;on&#95;Foreign&#95;Relations&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Council_on_Foreign_Relations-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>GM crops also provide a number of ecological benefits, if not used in excess.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-nytimes.com&#95;93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes.com-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Insect-resistant crops have proven to lower pesticide usage, therefore reducing the environmental impact of pesticides as a whole.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, opponents have objected to GM crops per se on several grounds, including environmental concerns, whether food produced from GM crops is safe, whether GM crops are needed to address the world's food needs, and economic concerns raised by the fact these organisms are subject to intellectual property law.
</p><p>Biotechnology has several applications in the realm of food security. Crops like <a href="/wiki/Golden_rice" title="Golden rice">Golden rice</a> are engineered to have higher nutritional content, and there is potential for food products with longer shelf lives.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Though not a form of agricultural biotechnology, vaccines can help prevent diseases found in animal agriculture. Additionally, agricultural biotechnology can expedite breeding processes in order to yield faster results and provide greater quantities of food.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Transgenic <a href="/wiki/Biofortification" title="Biofortification">biofortification</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cereal" title="Cereal">cereals</a> has been considered as a promising method to combat malnutrition in India and other countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Industrial">Industrial</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biotechnology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Industrial"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Industrial biotechnology (known mainly in Europe as white biotechnology) is the application of biotechnology for industrial purposes, including <a href="/wiki/Industrial_fermentation" title="Industrial fermentation">industrial fermentation</a>. It includes the practice of using <a href="/wiki/Cell_(biology)" title="Cell (biology)">cells</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Microorganism" title="Microorganism">microorganisms</a>, or components of cells like <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>, to generate <a href="/wiki/Industry_(manufacturing)" class="mw-redirect" title="Industry (manufacturing)">industrially</a> useful products in sectors such as chemicals, food and feed, detergents, paper and pulp, textiles and <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the current decades, significant progress has been done in creating <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified organisms (GMOs)</a> that enhance the diversity of applications and economical viability of industrial biotechnology. By using renewable raw materials to produce a variety of chemicals and fuels, industrial biotechnology is actively advancing towards lowering greenhouse gas emissions and moving away from a petrochemical-based economy.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Synthetic_biology" title="Synthetic biology">Synthetic biology</a> is considered one of the essential cornerstones in industrial biotechnology due to its financial and sustainable contribution to the manufacturing sector. Jointly biotechnology and synthetic biology play a crucial role in generating cost-effective products with <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmentally friendly">nature-friendly</a> features by using bio-based production instead of fossil-based.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Synthetic biology can be used to engineer <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model microorganisms</a>, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">Escherichia coli</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Genome_editing" title="Genome editing">genome editing</a> tools to enhance their ability to produce bio-based products, such as <a href="/wiki/Bioproduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Bioproduction">bioproduction</a> of medicines and <a href="/wiki/Biofuel" title="Biofuel">biofuels</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For instance, <i><a href="/wiki/Escherichia_coli" title="Escherichia coli">E. coli</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Saccharomyces_cerevisiae" title="Saccharomyces cerevisiae">Saccharomyces cerevisiae</a></i> in a consortium could be used as industrial microbes to produce precursors of the <a href="/wiki/Chemotherapeutic_agent" class="mw-redirect" title="Chemotherapeutic agent">chemotherapeutic agent</a> <a href="/wiki/Paclitaxel" title="Paclitaxel">paclitaxel</a> by applying the <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_engineering" title="Metabolic engineering">metabolic engineering</a> in a co-culture approach to exploit the benefits from the two microbes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another example of synthetic biology applications in industrial biotechnology is the re-engineering of the <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_pathway" title="Metabolic pathway">metabolic pathways</a> of <i>E. coli</i> by <a href="/wiki/CRISPR_gene_editing" title="CRISPR gene editing">CRISPR</a> and <a href="/wiki/CRISPR_interference" title="CRISPR interference">CRISPRi</a> systems toward the production of a chemical known as <a href="/wiki/1,4-Butanediol" title="1,4-Butanediol">1,4-butanediol</a>, which is used in fiber manufacturing. In order to produce 1,4-butanediol, the authors alter the metabolic regulation of the <i>Escherichia coli</i> by CRISPR to induce <a href="/wiki/Point_mutation" title="Point mutation">point mutation</a> in the <i>glt</i>A gene, <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockout" title="Gene knockout">knockout</a> of the <i>sad</i> gene, and <a href="/wiki/Gene_knock-in" title="Gene knock-in">knock-in</a> six genes (<i>cat</i>1, <i>suc</i>D, <i>4hbd</i>, <i>cat</i>2, <i>bld</i>, and <i>bdh</i>). Whereas CRISPRi system used to <a href="/wiki/Gene_knockdown" title="Gene knockdown">knockdown</a> the three competing genes (<i>gab</i>D, <i>ybg</i>C, and <i>tes</i>B) that affect the biosynthesis pathway of 1,4-butanediol. Consequently, the yield of 1,4-butanediol significantly increased from 0.9 to 1.8 g/L.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Environmental">Environmental</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biotechnology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Environmental"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Environmental_biotechnology" title="Environmental biotechnology">Environmental biotechnology</a> includes various disciplines that play an essential role in reducing environmental waste and providing <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmentally friendly">environmentally safe</a> processes, such as <a href="/wiki/Biofilter" title="Biofilter">biofiltration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradation</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The environment can be affected by biotechnologies, both positively and adversely. Vallero and others have argued that the difference between beneficial biotechnology (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Bioremediation" title="Bioremediation">bioremediation</a> is to clean up an oil spill or hazard chemical leak) versus the adverse effects stemming from biotechnological enterprises (e.g., flow of genetic material from transgenic organisms into wild strains) can be seen as applications and implications, respectively.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cleaning up environmental wastes is an example of an application of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_biotechnology" title="Environmental biotechnology">environmental biotechnology</a>; whereas <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">loss of biodiversity</a> or loss of containment of a harmful microbe are examples of environmental implications of biotechnology.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2024)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Many cities have installed <a href="/wiki/CityTrees" title="CityTrees">CityTrees</a>, which use biotechnology to filter pollutants from urban atmospheres.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Regulation">Regulation</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biotechnology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Regulation"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_genetic_engineering" title="Regulation of genetic engineering">Regulation of genetic engineering</a> and <a href="/wiki/Regulation_of_the_release_of_genetic_modified_organisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Regulation of the release of genetic modified organisms">Regulation of the release of genetic modified organisms</a></div>
<p>The regulation of genetic engineering concerns approaches taken by governments to assess and manage the <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology_risk" title="Biotechnology risk">risks</a> associated with the use of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> technology, and the development and release of genetically modified organisms (GMO), including <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_crops" title="Genetically modified crops">genetically modified crops</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_fish" title="Genetically modified fish">genetically modified fish</a>. There are differences in the regulation of GMOs between countries, with some of the most marked differences occurring between the US and Europe.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Regulation varies in a given country depending on the intended use of the products of the genetic engineering. For example, a crop not intended for food use is generally not reviewed by authorities responsible for food safety.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-PotatoPro&#95;110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PotatoPro-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The European Union differentiates between approval for cultivation within the EU and approval for import and processing. While only a few GMOs have been approved for cultivation in the EU a number of GMOs have been approved for import and processing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wesseler-2011&#95;111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wesseler-2011-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The cultivation of GMOs has triggered a debate about the coexistence of GM and non-GM crops. Depending on the coexistence regulations, incentives for the cultivation of GM crops differ.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Beckman-2011&#95;112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beckman-2011-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Database_for_the_GMOs_used_in_the_EU">Database for the GMOs used in the EU</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biotechnology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Database for the GMOs used in the EU"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/EUginius" title="EUginius">EUginius</a> (European GMO Initiative for a Unified Database System) database is intended to help companies, interested private users and competent authorities to find precise information on the presence, detection and identification of GMOs used in the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>. The information is provided in English.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Learning">Learning</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Biotechnology&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Learning"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Central_New_York_Biotech_Accelerator-entrance.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Central_New_York_Biotech_Accelerator-entrance.jpg/250px-Central_New_York_Biotech_Accelerator-entrance.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Central_New_York_Biotech_Accelerator-entrance.jpg/500px-Central_New_York_Biotech_Accelerator-entrance.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3600" data-file-height="2400" /></a><figcaption>Central New York Biotech Accelerator, <a href="/wiki/State_University_of_New_York_Upstate_Medical_University" title="State University of New York Upstate Medical University">Upstate Medical University</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>In 1988, after prompting from the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_General_Medical_Sciences" title="National Institute of General Medical Sciences">National Institute of General Medical Sciences</a> (<a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">National Institutes of Health</a>) (NIGMS) instituted a funding mechanism for biotechnology training. Universities nationwide compete for these funds to establish Biotechnology Training Programs (BTPs). Each successful application is generally funded for five years then must be competitively renewed. <a href="/wiki/Graduate_students" class="mw-redirect" title="Graduate students">Graduate students</a> in turn compete for acceptance into a BTP; if accepted, then stipend, tuition and health insurance support are provided for two or three years during the course of their <a href="/wiki/PhD" class="mw-redirect" title="PhD">PhD</a> thesis work. Nineteen institutions offer NIGMS supported BTPs.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Biotechnology training is also offered at undergraduate level and in community colleges.
</p>
<div style="clear:both;" class=""></div>
